### 7.2 Identity and Authentication Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.2.1 Password-Based Authentication](#password-based-authentication)
- [7.2.2 Two-Factor Authentication](#two-factor-authentication)
- [7.2.3 User Registration](#user-registration)
- [7.2.4 Password Reset](#password-reset)
- [7.2.5 Social Login](#social-login)

**Implemented controls:** Login and registration routes exist (`routes/login.ts`, `server.ts:408`); TOTP enrollment and verification are served at `routes/2fa.ts`; OAuth is handled in the Angular frontend at `frontend/src/app/oauth`.

**Assessment:** The authentication boundary is defeated at four independent layers. SQL string interpolation at `routes/login.ts:34` bypasses credential checking entirely. The OAuth adapter at `oauth.component.ts:30` derives a deterministic local password from the user's email, collapsing OAuth's separation-of-concerns guarantee. JWT verification at `lib/insecurity.ts:55` accepts algorithm substitution, allowing forged tokens with the public key as an HMAC secret. TOTP is opt-in and not enforced for admin accounts, so the possession factor adds no mandatory barrier. Each successful flow terminates in the server issuing a session token; the signing, validation, storage, and lifecycle of that token are described in [§7.3 Session and Token Controls](#73-session-and-token-controls).

<!-- §7.2 AUTH-MECHANISMS-FROZEN — deterministic inventory, pregenerator-owned. DO NOT EDIT. -->
**Authentication mechanisms (at a glance).** Every authentication mechanism detected on the application, its effective status, where it is assessed, and its linked findings. Controls are catalogued by domain, so JWT/session handling is assessed under [§7.3 Session and Token Controls](#73-session-and-token-controls) and password hashing under [§7.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection](#79-cryptography-secrets-and-data-protection).

| Mechanism | Status | Assessed in | Findings |
|----------------------|---------|-----------|------------------------------------------------|
| User registration | 🔴 Unsafe | [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🔴 [F-001](#f-001) — Systemic Mass Assignment of Privileged Fields — `server.ts:408`<br/>🔴 [F-012](#f-012) — Unauthenticated WebSocket Channel — `registerWebsocketEvents.ts:23`<br/>🔴 [F-042](#f-042) — Sensitive Routes Registered Without Authentication Middleware — `server.ts:310`<br/>🟡 [F-055](#f-055) — Notification Broadcast to Unauthorized Sockets — `registerWebsocketEvents.ts:29` |
| Password login | 🔴 Unsafe | [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-010](#f-010) — Missing Brute-Force Protection on Login — `server.ts:596` |
| Password reset / change | 🔴 Unsafe | [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-011](#f-011) — Weak Security-Question Password Reset — `routes/resetPassword.ts:41`<br/>🟠 [F-041](#f-041) — Password Change Without Current Password — `routes/changePassword.ts:39` |
| Password storage (hashing) | 🟠 High | [§7.9](#79-cryptography-secrets-and-data-protection) | 🟠 [F-025](#f-025) — Arbitrary Field Selection Leaks Password Hash — `routes/currentUser.ts:30`<br/>🟠 [F-026](#f-026) — Unsalted MD5 Password Hashing — `lib/insecurity.ts:41` |
| JWT / bearer-token session | 🔴 Unsafe | [§7.3](#73-session-and-token-controls) | 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — Insecure JWT Verification — `lib/insecurity.ts:55`<br/>🟠 [F-023](#f-023) — JWT session token stored in localStorage readable — `request.interceptor.ts:16` |
| Session-token storage | 🟠 High | [§7.3](#73-session-and-token-controls) | 🟠 [F-023](#f-023) — JWT session token stored in localStorage readable — `request.interceptor.ts:16` |
| Multi-factor authentication (TOTP / 2FA) | 🟡 Partial | [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🟠 [F-013](#f-013) — TOTP 2FA secret stored in plaintext — `models/user.ts:112` |
| OAuth / OIDC federated login | 🔴 Unsafe | [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) | 🔴 [F-003](#f-003) — OAuth password derived deterministically from email — `oauth.component.ts:30`<br/>🟠 [F-009](#f-009) — OAuth implicit flow leaks access_token in URL fragment — `oauth.component.ts:71` |

<!-- §7.2 AUTH-MECHANISMS-FROZEN END -->

<a id="password-based-authentication"></a>
#### 7.2.1 Password-Based Authentication

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the login query concatenates user input into raw SQL, making credential bypass trivial with a single injected quote; no rate limiting compounds the exposure.

`routes/login.ts:34` checks credentials by interpolating `req.body.email` directly into a `models.sequelize.query()` call against the Users table. Passwords are hashed with unsalted MD5 via `lib/insecurity.ts:41` before comparison. A successful login invokes `lib/insecurity.ts:issueAuthToken()`, which signs a `{id, email, role}` payload with the RSA private key and returns the encoded JWT to the caller.

The diagram shows the intended positive password-login path, from submitted credentials to the returned session token:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular Login Form
    participant API
    Note over API: POST /rest/user/login
    participant DB
    Note over DB: SQLite Users Table
    participant JWT
    Note over JWT: issueAuthToken()

    User->>SPA: Email and password
    SPA->>API: POST credentials
    API->>DB: Query Users WHERE email = input AND password = md5(input)
    DB-->>API: Matching user row
    API->>JWT: Sign payload with RSA key
    JWT-->>API: Encoded RS256 token
    API-->>SPA: "{ token, bid, email }"
```

**Security assessment**

Three independent weaknesses affect this path:

- `routes/login.ts:34` concatenates `req.body.email` into the SQL string; submitting `' OR 1=1--` returns the seeded admin row and grants admin access without a password.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:41` uses `crypto.createHash('md5').update(data).digest('hex')` - unsalted, single-pass MD5; a database dump immediately yields rainbow-table-recoverable credentials.
- `server.ts:596` registers the login route with no rate-limiting or lockout middleware, leaving brute-force unrestricted.

The login query that enables SQL injection authentication bypass is:

```ts
models.sequelize.query(`SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email || ''}' AND password = '${security.hash(req.body.password || '')}' AND deletedAt IS NULL`)
```

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-003 — OAuth password derived deterministically from email](#f-003) — the OAuth adapter derives a deterministic local password from the email address and sends it through this same login endpoint, bypassing any real credential factor.
- 🔴 [F-005 — Insecure JWT Verification](#f-005) — JWT verification at `lib/insecurity.ts:55` does not pin the algorithm, so a token signed with the public key as an HMAC secret passes verification; this affects every login path.
- 🟠 [F-010 — Missing Brute-Force Protection on Login](#f-010) — no rate-limiting or lockout middleware guards the login endpoint, making brute-force and credential-stuffing attacks unrestricted.

<a id="two-factor-authentication"></a><a id="two-factor-authentication-totp"></a><a id="totp-2fa-mfa"></a>
#### 7.2.2 TOTP/2FA/MFA

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - TOTP enrollment and verification are implemented, but 2FA is opt-in and bypassed by all non-password login paths.

TOTP enrollment and verification are handled by `routes/2fa.ts` using the `otplib` library. An enrolled user must supply a time-based one-time password as a second factor during login. The server-side `totpSecret` field on the User model gates this check.

The diagram shows the intended TOTP verification path for a user who has enrolled 2FA:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular Login Form
    participant API
    Note over API: POST /rest/user/login
    participant TwoFA
    Note over TwoFA: POST /rest/2fa/verify
    participant DB
    Note over DB: SQLite Users Table

    User->>SPA: Email and password
    SPA->>API: POST credentials
    API->>DB: Verify password hash
    DB-->>API: User row with totpSecret set
    API-->>SPA: Partial auth - TOTP required
    User->>SPA: 6-digit TOTP code
    SPA->>TwoFA: POST TOTP token
    TwoFA->>DB: Verify TOTP against totpSecret
    DB-->>TwoFA: Valid
    TwoFA-->>SPA: Full session JWT issued
```

**Security assessment**

TOTP is functional for enrolled users but not mandatory for any account class:

- 2FA is never enforced for admin accounts; an admin who has not opted in receives a full session token after password-only login.
- The OAuth flow (`oauth.component.ts:30`) derives a local password from the email address and calls the local login endpoint directly, bypassing the TOTP check for OAuth-authenticated users entirely.
- `models/user.ts:112` stores `totpSecret` as a plain `DataTypes.STRING` with no encryption hook; a SQLite dump yields TOTP seeds in cleartext.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-003 — OAuth password derived deterministically from email](#f-003) — the OAuth login path derives a local password and enters the local login flow, circumventing the TOTP gate for OAuth-registered accounts.
- 🔴 [F-005 — Insecure JWT Verification](#f-005) — algorithm-confusion in JWT verification allows forging a valid admin session token without passing any authentication factor, including 2FA.
- 🟠 [F-010 — Missing Brute-Force Protection on Login](#f-010) — the TOTP verification endpoint itself has no rate limiting, allowing offline enumeration of the 30-second window.

<a id="user-registration"></a>
#### 7.2.3 User Registration

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the auto-generated REST endpoint accepts arbitrary model fields, allowing self-registration with elevated role.

`POST /api/Users` is handled by the auto-generated `finale-rest` endpoint backed by the Sequelize User model. The pre-processing middleware at `server.ts:408` is intended to strip privileged fields before passing the payload to the ORM, but the allowlist is incomplete and the `role` field can be set by a self-registering user.

The diagram shows the intended self-registration path:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular Registration Form
    participant API
    Note over API: POST /api/Users
    participant Middleware
    Note over Middleware: server.ts:408 pre-handler
    participant DB
    Note over DB: SQLite Users Table

    User->>SPA: Email, password, profile fields
    SPA->>API: POST user payload
    API->>Middleware: Strip privileged fields
    Middleware->>DB: INSERT sanitized user row
    DB-->>API: Created user record
    API-->>SPA: 201 Created with user object
```

**Security assessment**

The middleware at `server.ts:408` does not fully sanitise the registration payload:

- `role` can be supplied as `admin` in the POST body and is persisted by the ORM; a self-registered account gains admin privileges without any approval step.
- `server.ts:310` registers several sensitive management routes (`/api/Users/:id`, `/api/Feedbacks/:id`) without an authentication middleware, making them reachable without a session token.
- `registerWebsocketEvents.ts:23` opens a Socket\.IO broadcast channel on every server-side connection without verifying a valid session, allowing unauthenticated clients to receive order and challenge notifications.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-001 — Systemic Mass Assignment of Privileged Fields](#f-001) — mass assignment of the `role` field during registration allows self-elevation to admin without any server-side guard.
- 🔴 [F-012 — Unauthenticated WebSocket Channel](#f-012) — Socket\.IO accepts connections without session-token verification, extending the registration surface to an unprotected real-time channel.
- 🔴 [F-042 — Sensitive Routes Registered Without Authentication Middleware](#f-042) — management-facing REST endpoints are registered without authentication middleware, allowing unauthenticated CRUD access to user and feedback records.
- 🟡 [F-055 — Notification Broadcast to Unauthorized Sockets](#f-055) — broadcast notifications sent to the shared Socket\.IO namespace reach unauthenticated sockets, leaking order-completion and challenge events.

<a id="password-reset"></a>
#### 7.2.4 Password Reset

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - password reset requires only a guessable security-question answer; no emailed token and no rate limit are in place.

`POST /rest/user/reset-password` in `routes/resetPassword.ts` accepts an email address, a security-question answer, and a new password. The route looks up the security question registered to the account, compares the supplied answer in a single step, and writes the new password hash if the answer matches. No time-limited token is sent to the registered email address.

The diagram shows the intended single-step reset path:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Reset Form
    participant API
    Note over API: POST /rest/user/reset-password
    participant DB
    Note over DB: Users + SecurityAnswers

    User->>SPA: Email, security answer, new password
    SPA->>API: POST reset payload
    API->>DB: Lookup security question by email
    DB-->>API: Registered security question
    API->>DB: Compare supplied answer
    DB-->>API: Answer matches
    API->>DB: UPDATE password hash
    DB-->>API: OK
    API-->>SPA: 200 - password updated
```

**Security assessment**

The reset mechanism lacks the two controls that would bound the blast radius of a guessed answer:

- `routes/resetPassword.ts:41` compares the security answer in a single unauthenticated call with no rate limit; common security questions (mother's maiden name, pet's name) are enumerable from social media and online data breaches.
- No emailed one-time token is issued, so a successful reset does not require access to the account's email inbox - defeating the canonical second-factor proof-of-account-ownership.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-011 — Weak Security-Question Password Reset](#f-011) — weak security-question answers can be enumerated or guessed, granting full account takeover via the unauthenticated reset endpoint.

<a id="social-login"></a><a id="social-login-oauth-oidc"></a>
#### 7.2.5 Social Login

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the OAuth adapter derives a deterministic local password from the email address, making the credential trivially computable by any party who knows the account's email.

`oauth.component.ts` implements OAuth as a frontend identity adapter, not a server-side authorization-code flow. After the Google OAuth redirect, the component reads the access token from the URL fragment, fetches the user's profile via the Google UserInfo endpoint, derives a local password as `btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))`, and calls `POST /rest/user/login` with that derived credential.

The diagram shows how the frontend OAuth adapter converts a Google identity into a local session:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA
    Note over SPA: oauth.component.ts
    participant Google
    Note over Google: Google UserInfo API
    participant API
    Note over API: POST /rest/user/login
    participant JWT
    Note over JWT: issueAuthToken()

    User->>SPA: Return from Google OAuth redirect (access token in URL fragment)
    SPA->>Google: GET /oauth2/v3/userinfo
    Google-->>SPA: Profile including email
    SPA->>SPA: Derive password = btoa(email reversed)
    SPA->>API: POST login with email plus derived password
    API->>JWT: Issue local session JWT
    JWT-->>API: Encoded RS256 token
    API-->>SPA: "{ token, bid, email }"
```

**Security assessment**

Two structural weaknesses make this adapter unsafe:

- `oauth.component.ts:30` computes `btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))` - a deterministic, reversible transform - as the account password. Any party who knows the email address can derive the password and authenticate through the local login endpoint without Google's involvement.
- `oauth.component.ts:71` uses the OAuth implicit flow, placing the `access_token` in the URL fragment. The token is visible to browser history, server logs, and any JavaScript running on the page before the fragment is cleared.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-003 — OAuth password derived deterministically from email](#f-003) — the deterministic password derivation collapses OAuth's identity guarantee; knowledge of an email address is sufficient to authenticate as that user.
- 🟠 [F-009 — OAuth implicit flow leaks access_token in URL fragment](#f-009) — the implicit flow exposes the Google access token in the URL fragment, where it is readable by any co-resident script or logged by browser history.

### 7.3 Session and Token Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.3.1 Session Token Issuance](#session-token-issuance)
- [7.3.2 Cookie Configuration](#cookie-configuration)

**Implemented controls:** `lib/insecurity.ts:20-55` issues and verifies JWTs using RS256; `server.ts` applies `express-jwt` middleware on protected routes; `server.ts:289` initialises `cookieParser` with a hardcoded signing secret.

**Assessment:** This application uses a single locally-signed token format (commonly called JWT) for every authenticated session, regardless of the login flow in [§7.2 Identity and Authentication Controls](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) that established it. The sub-sections below trace one token through its lifecycle: signing on issuance, validation on every protected request, storage in the browser, and cookie-based session configuration. Two structural defects break both ends of the lifecycle: the signing key is hardcoded in the repository (`lib/insecurity.ts:21`), making offline forgery trivial for anyone with a copy of the source; and the token is stored in `localStorage` (`request.interceptor.ts:16`), where any XSS payload can read and exfiltrate it.

<a id="session-token-issuance"></a><a id="session-token-issuance-jwt-based"></a>
#### 7.3.1 Session Token Issuance

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the RSA private key used to sign every session token is hardcoded in source and committed to the public repository; the verifier accepts algorithm substitution.

`lib/insecurity.ts` exports `privateKey` (a 1024-bit PEM block at line 21) and `publicKey` (line 34) as string literals. `issueAuthToken()` at line 52 signs the payload `{ id, email, role }` with RS256 using this hardcoded key and sets a one-hour expiry. `verify()` at line 55 calls `jws.verify(token, publicKey)` without an `algorithms:` constraint, so any algorithm named in the token header is trusted.

The diagram shows the positive JWT issuance path from a successful credential check to the encoded token returned to the browser:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant API as Login Route
    participant Issuer
    Note over Issuer: issueAuthToken()
    participant Key
    Note over Key: RSA Private Key (lib/insecurity.ts:21)

    User->>API: Successful credential verification
    API->>Issuer: Sign payload with id, email, role
    Issuer->>Key: Load hardcoded PEM bytes
    Key-->>Issuer: 1024-bit RSA private key
    Issuer-->>API: Encoded RS256 JWT (1-hour expiry)
    API-->>User: "{ token, bid, email }"
```

**Security assessment**

The private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` is published in the public OWASP Juice Shop repository, so any party who clones the repo can sign arbitrary JWTs with `role: admin` and have them accepted by `verify()`. The verifier's missing `algorithms:` allowlist adds a second bypass: an attacker can change the header algorithm to `HS256`, sign the token with the public key as the HMAC secret, and the verifier accepts it because `jws.verify()` follows the header rather than pinning the algorithm.

The hardcoded key material that undermines the issuance boundary is embedded as a literal:

```ts
export const privateKey = `----**** (31 chars)
MIICXAIBAAKBgQDNwqLEe9rrHa+zzA...`
```

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-023 — JWT session token stored in localStorage readable](#f-023) — the issued JWT is written to `localStorage` by `request.interceptor.ts:16`, where any XSS payload on the SPA can read and exfiltrate it; the token lifecycle is broken at the storage step as well as the issuance step.

<a id="cookie-configuration"></a>
#### 7.3.2 Cookie Configuration

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the cookie signing secret is hardcoded as `'kekse'` in the application source; cookie flags `HttpOnly`, `Secure`, and `SameSite` are not set on the session cookie.

`server.ts:289` initialises `cookieParser` with the literal secret `'kekse'`. This secret signs cookies used by the `remember-me` and session mechanisms. Because the session JWT is stored in `localStorage` rather than an `HttpOnly` cookie, the browser's same-origin isolation for cookies does not protect the primary session credential.

**Security assessment**

Two gaps affect cookie-level session security:

- The `cookieParser('kekse')` secret at `server.ts:289` is publicly committed; an attacker who reads the source can forge signed cookie values.
- The primary session token (JWT) is not set as a cookie at all - it lives in `localStorage` - so `HttpOnly`, `Secure`, and `SameSite` protections are structurally unavailable for the credential that guards all authenticated routes.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-023 — JWT session token stored in localStorage readable](#f-023) — JWT storage in `localStorage` (rather than an `HttpOnly Secure SameSite=Strict` cookie) means the session credential is readable by any script on the page, including XSS payloads.

### 7.4 Authorization Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.4.1 Role-Based Access Control](#role-based-access-control)

**Implemented controls:** `lib/insecurity.ts` exports `isAuthorized()` and `isAccounting()` middleware; route-level guards are applied selectively across `routes/userProfile.ts` and `routes/basketItems.ts`.

**Assessment:** Role-based access control exists as middleware but is applied inconsistently. Several management-facing routes are registered without any authentication middleware (`server.ts:310`). Angular route guards (`app.guard.ts:52`) enforce access control only in the browser without a matching server-side check, so direct API calls bypass them entirely. Object-level authorization is absent on address and basket endpoints, allowing one authenticated user to read or modify another's records.

<a id="role-based-access-control"></a>
#### 7.4.1 Role-Based Access Control

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - role checks exist in middleware but are missing from several management-facing routes and absent entirely for object-level resource ownership.

`lib/insecurity.ts` provides `isAuthorized()` (verifies the JWT `role` claim) and `isAccounting()` (checks for an `accounting` role) as Express middleware. Routes such as `routes/userProfile.ts` and `routes/basketItems.ts` apply these guards on protected paths. Angular's `AuthGuard` at `app.guard.ts:52` provides a client-side barrier on navigation but makes no server call.

**Security assessment**

Three gaps allow authorization to be bypassed:

- `app.guard.ts:52` is the only control on several admin navigation paths; direct HTTP requests to the underlying API endpoints ignore client-side guards, letting any authenticated user reach admin-only resources.
- `server.ts:310` registers management routes (`/api/Users/:id`, `/api/Feedbacks/:id`) without an authentication middleware entry, so unauthenticated callers can read and modify these records.
- Address endpoints at `routes/address.ts:11` accept a user-supplied record ID without verifying that the requesting session owns the record, enabling horizontal privilege escalation between accounts.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-001 — Systemic Mass Assignment of Privileged Fields](#f-001) — mass assignment of the `role` field during registration allows self-elevation to admin, bypassing the role-check middleware entirely.
- 🟠 [F-002 — Client-side-only route guards decide admin access in the](#f-002) — client-side-only route guards in `app.guard.ts:52` have no server-side equivalent, allowing direct API access to admin endpoints without a role check.
- 🔴 [F-007 — Insecure Direct Object Reference](#f-007) — missing object-level ownership checks on address and basket routes allow any authenticated user to read or modify another user's records.

### 7.5 Query Construction and Data Access Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.5.1 SQL Query Parameterization](#sql-query-parameterization)

**Implemented controls:** Sequelize ORM backs most model operations; `routes/login.ts:34` and `routes/search.ts:23` bypass the ORM and call `models.sequelize.query()` with raw string interpolation.

**Assessment:** Sequelize is used as the primary data access layer for most routes, but the two highest-traffic query paths - login and product search - bypass the ORM and concatenate user-supplied strings directly into SQL. A third path at `routes/showProductReviews.ts:36` uses MarsDB with a `$where` JavaScript evaluation clause that accepts user-controlled structure. Together these three routes offer three SQL/NoSQL injection entry points on the most commonly exercised API surface.

<a id="sql-query-parameterization"></a>
#### 7.5.1 SQL Query Parameterization

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - login and product-search routes interpolate user-controlled input directly into raw SQL strings; no parameterized binding or ORM call protects these paths.

Sequelize models back most relational data access, but `routes/login.ts` and `routes/search.ts` call `models.sequelize.query()` directly with template-literal string interpolation. The product-review update path uses MarsDB and passes `req.params.id` into a `$where` JavaScript evaluator where a scalar identifier should be required.

The vulnerable product-search query illustrates the raw string construction pattern:

```ts
models.sequelize.query(
  `SELECT * FROM Products WHERE ((name LIKE '%${criteria}%' OR description LIKE '%${criteria}%') AND deletedAt IS NULL) ORDER BY name`
)
```

**Security assessment**

Three query-construction weaknesses share the same root cause - user input reaches a query parser without binding:

- `routes/login.ts:34`: `req.body.email` is interpolated directly; `' OR 1=1--` bypasses password comparison and returns the seeded admin row.
- `routes/search.ts:23`: `req.query.q` is interpolated into a LIKE clause; UNION-based injection can extract arbitrary table data.
- `routes/showProductReviews.ts:36`: `req.params.id` is concatenated into `{ $where: 'this._id == ' + id }`, allowing `$where` JavaScript evaluation with attacker-controlled logic.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-006 — SQL Injection in login query](#f-006) — SQL injection in the login query allows authentication bypass without a valid password.
- 🔴 [F-008 — SQL injection](#f-008) in the product search query enables data exfiltration from any table in the SQLite database.
- 🔴 [F-019 — NoSQL Injection](#f-019) — NoSQL injection in the product review route allows `$where` JavaScript evaluation, enabling data access and server-side logic manipulation.

### 7.6 Input Boundary Validation Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.6.1 Validation Approach](#validation-approach)
- [7.6.2 Input Sanitization and Validation](#input-sanitization-and-validation)

**Implemented controls:** `sanitize-html@1.4.2` is declared in `package.json`; individual routes apply ad-hoc checks but no shared validation middleware is applied across the API surface.

**Assessment:** No framework-level request schema validation (`e.g`. `express-validator`, Joi, Zod) is applied uniformly across the Express route table. Each route performs its own ad-hoc checks, leading to inconsistent boundaries. File-upload routes lack parser-size limits and type-validation depth, as demonstrated by the YAML-bomb exhaustion path at `routes/fileUpload.ts:109`. The `sanitize-html` version in use predates several critical bypass patches.

<a id="validation-approach"></a>
#### 7.6.1 Validation Approach

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - there is no shared request-validation middleware; each route performs ad-hoc input checks, leaving several paths unguarded against structured input abuse.

Request validation in this codebase is route-local - each handler in `routes/` applies whatever checks its author included. There is no centralized schema-validation library applied at the router or middleware layer. Some routes validate the presence of required fields; most do not constrain type, length, or structure.

**Security assessment**

The absence of a uniform validation layer produces two measurable gaps:

- Upload endpoints (`routes/fileUpload.ts`) accept arbitrary MIME types and do not cap payload size at the parser level; a specially crafted YAML file can exhaust server memory before any application-level check runs.
- No input constraint is applied to numeric IDs, JSON object keys, or URL parameters at the middleware layer, so injection-capable routes (SQL, NoSQL, path traversal) receive unsanitized values and must defend themselves individually - a defense that has failed on the login, search, and review routes.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-059 — YAML-bomb resource exhaustion on upload](#f-059) — YAML-bomb resource exhaustion at `routes/fileUpload.ts:109` demonstrates that the upload path lacks both MIME-type depth checks and parser-level size limits.

<a id="input-sanitization-and-validation"></a>
#### 7.6.2 Input Sanitization and Validation

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - the declared sanitizer is an outdated version with known allowlist-bypass patterns; server-side sanitization is applied inconsistently.

`sanitize-html@1.4.2` is listed in `package.json` as the HTML sanitizer. It is called in the product-review route before storing user content. Angular's template engine applies output escaping by default on data-bound values, but several components use `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` to pass HTML through unsanitized to the DOM.

**Security assessment**

The sanitization layer has gaps at both the server and client sides:

- `sanitize-html@1.4.2` is several major versions behind the current release and predates allowlist improvements that closed several bypass techniques; the version in use cannot be assumed safe against modern injection payloads.
- Inconsistent server-side call sites mean some product-content routes store unsanitized HTML that later renders in Angular components using trusted-HTML APIs.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-059 — YAML-bomb resource exhaustion on upload](#f-059) — the outdated sanitizer version and ad-hoc call-site coverage leave user-supplied HTML content partially unprotected against injection and stored-XSS.

### 7.7 Output Encoding and Rendering Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.7.1 XSS Prevention](#xss-prevention)

**Implemented controls:** Angular template binding applies automatic contextual escaping for data-bound expressions; `server.ts` references `helmet.xssFilter()` in a commented-out block; `routes/createProductReviews.ts` calls `sanitize-html` before persistence.

**Assessment:** Angular's default template escaping is the primary XSS defence - it is sound for data-bound expressions but bypassed wherever `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` is called. The Helmet XSS filter (`helmet.xssFilter()`) that would add an `X-XSS-Protection` response header is commented out in `server.ts`, removing one layer of browser-level defence. Without a Content-Security-Policy header (absent - see [§7.8](#78-browser-and-cross-origin-controls)), a stored-XSS payload runs with full script privileges.

<a id="xss-prevention"></a>
#### 7.7.1 XSS Prevention

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - Angular's template escaping protects most data-bound paths, but `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` is used in the search-result component, creating a DOM-injection sink that stored payloads reach.

Angular's default template engine treats data-bound values as text nodes, not HTML, providing automatic XSS prevention for the majority of rendered content. `routes/createProductReviews.ts` also passes review content through `sanitize-html` before writing to the database.

**Security assessment**

The escaping layer is bypassed at one confirmed sink:

- `search-result.component.ts:143` uses `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` to render product descriptions as raw HTML. A product description containing a `<script>` tag or event-handler attribute runs immediately when the search result is displayed.
- `server.ts` includes `helmet.xssFilter()` only in a commented-out block; the `X-XSS-Protection: 1; mode=block` header is therefore never sent, removing the legacy browser-level XSS filter.
- No Content-Security-Policy header restricts which scripts may execute (see [§7.8.2 Security Headers](#security-headers)), so a stored XSS payload runs without any origin constraint.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-015 — Cross-Site Scripting](#f-015) — `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` at `search-result.component.ts:143` creates an exploitable DOM injection sink that stored product-description payloads reach directly.

### 7.8 Browser and Cross-Origin Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.8.1 CORS Configuration](#cors-configuration)
- [7.8.2 Security Headers](#security-headers)

**Implemented controls:** `server.ts:182-183` applies `app.use(cors())` globally with no origin allowlist; `server.ts` applies `helmet.noSniff()` and `helmet.frameguard()` but not CSP or HSTS.

**Assessment:** The CORS policy reflects every origin unconditionally, meaning cross-origin JavaScript on any domain can make credentialed requests to the API. No Content-Security-Policy header is set, so inline scripts and scripts from any origin are permitted. Clickjacking protection via `frameguard` is in place, and `noSniff` prevents MIME-type sniffing, but these two headers are the only browser-security controls applied.

<a id="cors-configuration"></a>
#### 7.8.1 CORS Configuration

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - `app.use(cors())` with no origin constraint reflects every `Origin` header, allowing cross-origin credentialed requests from any domain.

`server.ts:182-183` applies Express CORS middleware with a bare `app.use(cors())` call followed by `app.options('*', cors())` for preflight. No `origin:` allowlist is passed, so the middleware reflects the incoming `Origin` header as `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` for every request.

**Security assessment**

The wildcard-equivalent CORS policy means:

- Any web page on the internet can make credentialed (`withCredentials: true`) cross-origin requests to the Juice Shop API and read the response. Combined with the JWT stored in `localStorage`, this gives an attacker-controlled page full API access as the victim user.
- `server.ts:183`: `app.use(cors())` reflects the caller's `Origin` rather than restricting to a configured SPA origin, defeating the browser's same-origin isolation for the API surface.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-050 — Wildcard CORS and missing CSP/HSTS response headers](#f-050) — wildcard CORS at `server.ts:183` combined with missing CSP and HSTS headers removes the browser-level origin boundary protecting the API from cross-site script requests.

<a id="security-headers"></a><a id="security-headers-csp-hsts-x-frame"></a>
#### 7.8.2 Security Headers

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `helmet.noSniff()` and `helmet.frameguard()` are applied, but Content-Security-Policy and HSTS are absent.

Helmet is initialised in `server.ts` with `helmet.noSniff()` (sets `X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff`) and `helmet.frameguard({action: 'sameorigin'})` (sets `X-Frame-Options: SAMEORIGIN`). These two headers are the extent of the security-header configuration.

**Security assessment**

Two high-value headers are missing:

- No `Content-Security-Policy` header is sent; inline scripts and scripts from any external origin execute freely, amplifying any stored or reflected XSS payload.
- No `Strict-Transport-Security` header is set; browsers do not enforce HTTPS after the first connection, leaving the session token exposed on any HTTP response.
- `helmet.xssFilter()` is referenced in a commented-out block - the `X-XSS-Protection` header is therefore never emitted.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-050 — Wildcard CORS and missing CSP/HSTS response headers](#f-050) — the missing CSP and HSTS headers, combined with wildcard CORS, remove the three browser-level controls that would limit the blast radius of a stored-XSS or MITM attack.

### 7.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.9.1 Secret and Key Management](#secret-and-key-management)
- [7.9.2 Data Encryption at Rest](#data-encryption-at-rest)

**Implemented controls:** RS256 is the intended JWT signing algorithm (`lib/insecurity.ts:52`); cookie parser is initialised with a signing secret (`server.ts:289`); no at-rest encryption is applied to the SQLite store or the payment-card model.

**Assessment:** Three secrets are hardcoded as string literals in source: the RSA private key (`lib/insecurity.ts:21`), the cookie signing secret (`server.ts:289`), and the HMAC key for security answers (`models/securityAnswer.ts:45`). All three are in the public repository. The password hashing algorithm is unsalted MD5 - a single fast hash with no work factor. Payment card numbers are stored in cleartext in the `Cards` model and the SQLite file is not encrypted, meaning a database dump yields full PAN data directly.

<a id="secret-and-key-management"></a><a id="secret-management"></a>
#### 7.9.1 Secret and Key Management

⚠ **Anti-pattern:** Secrets hardcoded in source

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - three secrets (RSA signing key, cookie secret, HMAC key) are hardcoded as string literals in committed source files and are publicly available in the repository.

`lib/insecurity.ts:21` embeds the 1024-bit RSA private key as a PEM literal. `server.ts:289` passes `'kekse'` as the cookie-signing secret to `cookieParser`. `models/securityAnswer.ts:45` uses a hardcoded HMAC key to hash security-question answers before storage.

**Security assessment**

All three secrets are published in the OWASP Juice Shop public repository:

- `lib/insecurity.ts:21`: the RSA private key - anyone who clones the repo can sign arbitrary JWTs with `role: admin` and have them accepted by the verifier.
- `server.ts:289`: the cookie secret `'kekse'` - an attacker can forge signed cookie values presented to any cookie-dependent session check.
- `models/securityAnswer.ts:45`: the hardcoded HMAC key means security-answer hashes are preimage-computable; a dump of the `SecurityAnswers` table can be reversed to cleartext answers.

The RSA key literal that undermines token security is in the committed source:

```ts
export const privateKey = `----**** (31 chars)
MIICXAIBAAKBgQDNwqLEe9rrHa+zzA...
-----END RSA PRIVATE KEY-----`
```

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-004 — Hardcoded RSA Signing Key](#f-004) — the hardcoded RSA private key allows offline JWT forgery for any user including admin accounts, without requiring server access.
- 🟠 [F-013 — TOTP 2FA secret stored in plaintext](#f-013) — the TOTP secret is stored as a plain string in the User model; a database dump exposes TOTP seeds that allow permanent second-factor bypass.
- 🔴 [F-037 — Hardcoded BIP-39 mnemonic in source](#f-037) — the hardcoded BIP-39 mnemonic in `routes/checkKeys.ts:10` exposes the Web3 wallet seed to anyone with repository access.

<a id="data-encryption-at-rest"></a>
#### 7.9.2 Data Encryption at Rest

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - no at-rest encryption is applied to the SQLite database file, the payment-card table, or the TOTP secret column.

The application stores all persistent data in a SQLite file at `data/juiceshop.sqlite`. The Sequelize initialisation at `models/index.ts:41` opens the database with no encryption extensions (`e.g`., SQLCipher). The `Cards` model (`models/card.ts:38`) declares the `cardNum` field as a plain `DataTypes.INTEGER` with no encryption hook.

**Security assessment**

A physical copy of the database file yields sensitive data in cleartext:

- `models/card.ts:38` stores full payment card numbers as plaintext integers; a database dump immediately yields card data without any key-derivation step.
- `models/index.ts:41` opens the SQLite file unencrypted; file-system access (`e.g`. via path-traversal or backup exposure) yields the complete database including password hashes, TOTP secrets, and payment data.
- `models/user.ts:112` stores TOTP secrets as `DataTypes.STRING` with no encryption hook, so a database dump yields TOTP seeds directly alongside hashed passwords.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-004 — Hardcoded RSA Signing Key](#f-004) — the hardcoded signing key means any database-level access combined with JWT forgery yields full admin takeover with no further obstacles.
- 🟠 [F-013 — TOTP 2FA secret stored in plaintext](#f-013) — the plaintext TOTP secret column means a single database dump discloses permanent second-factor bypass material for every enrolled user.
- 🔴 [F-037 — Hardcoded BIP-39 mnemonic in source](#f-037) — the hardcoded BIP-39 mnemonic provides wallet-seed recovery material without requiring database access, only repository read access.

### 7.10 File Parser and Outbound Request Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Missing

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.10.1 File Upload Validation](#file-upload-validation)
- [7.10.2 Server-Side Request Forgery (SSRF) Prevention](#server-side-request-forgery-ssrf-prevention)
- [7.10.3 XML/XXE Prevention](#xmlxxe-prevention)

**Implemented controls:** `routes/fileUpload.ts` uses `multer` for multipart handling; `routes/b2bOrder.ts` passes XML input to `lib/xml.ts` using `libxmljs2`; `routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts` calls `fetch(url)` on a user-supplied URL without host validation.

**Assessment:** Three distinct outbound/parser attack surfaces exist with no meaningful controls in place. The upload path at `/file-upload` uses `multer` but trusts archive entry paths verbatim, enabling zip-slip path traversal. XML uploads at the B2B order endpoint parse with external-entity loading enabled, allowing local file disclosure. The profile-image URL endpoint calls `fetch()` on a user-supplied URL without scheme or host validation, enabling SSRF to internal services. Redirect validation at `routes/redirect.ts:19` uses a substring allowlist that is bypassable with crafted URLs.

<a id="file-upload-validation"></a>
#### 7.10.1 File Upload Validation

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `multer` enforces a file-size limit but does not validate archive entry paths or restrict by file extension; zip-slip path traversal writes files outside the upload directory.

`routes/fileUpload.ts` accepts multipart uploads and passes ZIP archives to `extractZipBuffer()`. Each archive entry's `entry.path` value is taken verbatim and streamed to `fs.createWriteStream(uploadPath + entry.path)` without any path-containment check. The memories endpoint at `server.ts:701` validates uploads by content-type string only, not by file content.

**Security assessment**

Two gaps in the upload pipeline allow attacker-controlled file placement:

- `routes/fileUpload.ts:33`: `entry.path` from a crafted ZIP archive (containing `../../server.ts`) is used directly in `fs.createWriteStream()`, overwriting arbitrary files in the server's working directory.
- `server.ts:701` (`/rest/memories` upload) accepts files whose `Content-Type` header matches an image type, without reading the file magic bytes; any file can be stored with a forged MIME header.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-014 — Open redirect](#f-014) — the open-redirect at `routes/redirect.ts:19` uses a substring allowlist that can be bypassed with a crafted URL containing an allowlisted domain as a path prefix.
- 🟠 [F-018 — XXE external-entity processing on XML upload](#f-018) — XXE-enabled XML parsing at `lib/xml.ts:37` processes archive-embedded XML files with external entity resolution active.
- 🟠 [F-021 — Path Traversal](#f-021) — zip-slip path traversal at `routes/fileUpload.ts:33` allows archive entries to write files to arbitrary paths outside the designated upload directory.

<a id="server-side-request-forgery-ssrf-prevention"></a>
#### 7.10.2 Server-Side Request Forgery (SSRF) Prevention

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - the profile-image upload endpoint calls `fetch()` on a user-supplied URL with no scheme restriction, host allowlist, or DNS-rebinding protection.

`routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24` accepts `req.body.imageUrl` from an authenticated user and calls `await fetch(url)` directly. No validation of the URL scheme, host, or IP address range is performed before the outbound HTTP request is made.

**Security assessment**

The absence of any URL validation on the fetch call enables full SSRF:

- An authenticated user POSTs `imageUrl: "http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/"` to `/profile/image/url`; the server makes the request and the response body (containing cloud-provider instance metadata) is stored as the user's avatar.
- Internal service URLs, private network addresses, and local file paths reachable via `file://` are equally accessible, enabling reconnaissance of internal services from the server's network position.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-014 — Open redirect](#f-014) — the open-redirect at `routes/redirect.ts:19` compounds the SSRF risk by providing an attacker-controlled forwarding hop through the application server.
- 🟠 [F-018 — XXE external-entity processing on XML upload](#f-018) — XXE at the B2B XML endpoint provides a second outbound-request vector via external-entity resolution, independent of the SSRF path.
- 🟠 [F-021 — Path Traversal](#f-021) — path traversal on upload provides a third outbound-to-write path for files placed outside the upload directory.

<a id="xmlxxe-prevention"></a>
#### 7.10.3 XML/XXE Prevention

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - `libxmljs2` parses uploaded XML with `XML_PARSE_NOENT` and `XML_PARSE_DTDLOAD` enabled, allowing DOCTYPE-declared external entities to read local files.

`lib/xml.ts:37` calls `parseXmlString()` with `{ noent: true }`, which instructs `libxmljs2` to resolve entity references including filesystem paths declared in a DOCTYPE. The B2B order route at `routes/b2bOrder.ts` forwards uploaded XML buffers to this parser without stripping the DOCTYPE first.

The `noent: true` option that enables external entity resolution is set unconditionally:

```ts
const parsed = libxmljs.parseXmlString(xmlString, { noent: true, noblanks: true })
```

**Security assessment**

The parser configuration reads local files on behalf of the attacker:

- An attacker uploads a crafted XML file containing `<!DOCTYPE foo [<!ENTITY xxe SYSTEM "file:///etc/passwd">]><foo>&xxe;</foo>`; the parser resolves the entity and the file contents appear in the parsed document, which the application echoes back in the response.
- `XML_PARSE_DTDLOAD` allows schema fetches from remote URLs in addition to local files, enabling blind SSRF through the XML parser as a second outbound-request path.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-014 — Open redirect](#f-014) — open redirect at `routes/redirect.ts:19` can be combined with XXE-based SSRF to chain server-side requests through the redirect endpoint.
- 🟠 [F-018 — XXE external-entity processing on XML upload](#f-018) — XXE at `lib/xml.ts:37` with `noent: true` allows local file disclosure and blind SSRF via DOCTYPE-declared external entities.
- 🟠 [F-021 — Path Traversal](#f-021) — path traversal on the file upload route provides a complementary write primitive that can be chained with XXE-based file reads.

### 7.11 Operations Runtime and Supply Chain Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Missing

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.11.1 Dependency Management and Supply Chain](#dependency-management-and-supply-chain)
- [7.11.2 Logging and Monitoring](#logging-and-monitoring)
- [7.11.3 Automated SCA scanning](#automated-sca-scanning)
- [7.11.4 Automated dependency updates](#automated-dependency-updates)
- [7.11.5 Lockfile hygiene](#lockfile-hygiene)

**Implemented controls:** `Dockerfile` generates a CycloneDX SBOM via `cyclonedx-npm`; `lib/logger.ts` provides structured Winston logging; `server.ts` applies Morgan HTTP request logging; `.dependabot/config.yml` exists but uses the deprecated v1 schema.

**Assessment:** Several supply-chain controls are configured but non-functional. Dependabot uses a v1 YAML schema that GitHub no longer processes, so no automated pull requests are opened for outdated packages. `.npmrc` sets `package-lock=false`, disabling the lockfile that would pin transitive dependency versions. The Dockerfile base image is referenced by a floating tag (`node:20`) rather than a content digest, allowing the build to silently pull a different image on re-run. Authentication events (login success, login failure, password reset) are not written to the structured log, making anomaly detection on auth events impossible.

<a id="dependency-management-and-supply-chain"></a>
#### 7.11.1 Dependency Management and Supply Chain

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - a CycloneDX SBOM is generated at build time, but the lockfile is disabled and the base image is pinned to a mutable tag, undermining build reproducibility.

`Dockerfile` runs `cyclonedx-npm --output-file bom.xml` to generate a software bill of materials during the Docker build. `.dependabot/config.yml` declares package-update schedules. `.npmrc` sets `package-lock=false`.

**Security assessment**

Two configuration choices break dependency determinism:

- `.npmrc:1` sets `package-lock=false`, meaning every `npm install` resolves the latest satisfying version within declared ranges; a new transitive dependency version is silently introduced on each build without any diff visible in the repository.
- `Dockerfile:1` references the base image as `node:20` without a content digest; a new image pushed to that tag at the registry becomes the build base without any repository change or review.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-016 — Npm lifecycle scripts execute on CI/Docker install](#f-016) — npm lifecycle scripts execute on every `npm install` in CI and Docker builds, allowing a compromised dependency's `postinstall` script to run arbitrary commands in the build environment.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Missing Security Event Logging](#f-022) — missing security-event logging means supply-chain compromise events (unexpected package execution, unusual network calls) are not captured in the audit trail.
- 🟠 [F-028 — Base image must be digest-pinned](#f-028) — the floating base-image tag allows a malicious image pushed to `node:20` to become the build base, introducing OS-level supply-chain risk.

<a id="logging-and-monitoring"></a>
#### 7.11.2 Logging and Monitoring

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - Winston structured logging and Morgan HTTP request logging are in place, but authentication events and security-relevant actions are not written to the structured log.

`lib/logger.ts` configures Winston with file and console transports. `server.ts` applies Morgan HTTP middleware, logging each incoming request method, path, and status code. These two layers cover general request traffic.

**Security assessment**

The logging stack misses the security-event categories most useful for intrusion detection:

- `routes/login.ts:18` does not call `logger` on authentication success or failure; login events are visible only in Morgan's request log (method + status code) with no username, IP address, or failure reason captured in a structured field.
- Password reset requests and password changes similarly produce no structured log entry; account-takeover attempts are invisible to log-based alerting.
- No correlation ID is propagated across log entries for a given session, making forensic reconstruction of an attack chain across multiple requests impractical.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-016 — Npm lifecycle scripts execute on CI/Docker install](#f-016) — the absence of npm-script execution logging means a compromised `postinstall` hook runs without any structured record that would surface it in a SIEM.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Missing Security Event Logging](#f-022) — missing security-event logging at `routes/login.ts:18` means authentication failures and successes produce no structured entry, leaving brute-force and credential-stuffing attacks undetectable from logs.
- 🟠 [F-028 — Base image must be digest-pinned](#f-028) — the floating base image means image-layer provenance is not captured in the build log; attestation of which OS packages were present at build time is impossible.

<a id="automated-sca-scanning"></a>
#### 7.11.3 Automated SCA scanning

**Status:** 🟢 Adequate - a CycloneDX SBOM is generated at Docker build time, providing a structured inventory of package versions and their known vulnerabilities.

A Software Composition Analysis artefact is produced during every Docker image build: `Dockerfile` runs `cyclonedx-npm --output-file bom.xml`, which produces a CycloneDX-format SBOM listing all installed npm packages and their declared versions. This SBOM can be consumed by vulnerability scanners that compare package versions against CVE databases.

**Security assessment**

The SBOM generation step is sound as far as it goes. The artefact is produced from the resolved `node_modules` tree inside the Docker build context, so it reflects what is actually installed rather than what `package.json` declares. The limitation is that the SBOM is only generated at Docker build time; a developer running the application outside Docker receives no SCA output. Vulnerability alerting on the SBOM output is not wired into the CI workflow.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-016 — Npm lifecycle scripts execute on CI/Docker install](#f-016) — the CycloneDX SBOM captures package names and versions but does not flag npm lifecycle script execution risk; the `postinstall` attack surface is outside its scope.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Missing Security Event Logging](#f-022) — vulnerability findings from SBOM scans are not correlated with runtime log events, so a known-exploited CVE in a loaded package produces no alert.
- 🟠 [F-028 — Base image must be digest-pinned](#f-028) — the SBOM covers npm packages but not the OS packages in the `node:20` base image; OS-level CVEs are not captured in this artefact.

<a id="automated-dependency-updates"></a>
#### 7.11.4 Automated dependency updates

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - the `.dependabot/config.yml` file uses the deprecated v1 schema that GitHub no longer processes; no automated pull requests are opened for outdated packages.

Automated dependency update tooling keeps package versions current by opening pull requests when upstream maintainers publish security patches, reducing the window between vulnerability disclosure and patch deployment. `.dependabot/config.yml` declares update schedules for `npm` and `docker`, indicating that automated updates were once intended.

**Security assessment**

The Dependabot configuration is structurally present but functionally inert:

- `.dependabot/config.yml:1` uses the v1 schema (`version: 1` key); GitHub's Dependabot service requires the v2 schema (`version: 2`) and silently ignores v1 files. No automated PRs are opened despite the configuration file's presence.
- As a result, known-vulnerable package versions in `package.json` accumulate without automated notification; manual review is the only mechanism for catching outdated transitive dependencies.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-016 — Npm lifecycle scripts execute on CI/Docker install](#f-016) — without automated update PRs, vulnerable npm lifecycle script execution risks from outdated packages go unpatched until manual review catches them.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Missing Security Event Logging](#f-022) — the inert Dependabot configuration means no structured audit trail exists for dependency-update decisions or deferred patches.
- 🟠 [F-028 — Base image must be digest-pinned](#f-028) — the base image floating-tag issue would have been surfaced by a functioning Dependabot Docker dependency check; the inert v1 config leaves it unmonitored.

<a id="lockfile-hygiene"></a>
#### 7.11.5 Lockfile hygiene

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - `.npmrc` sets `package-lock=false`, disabling the `package-lock.json` that would pin every transitive dependency to a specific content-addressed version.

A lockfile records the exact resolved version and registry checksum of every direct and transitive npm dependency. When present and committed, it ensures that `npm ci` in CI and Docker builds installs the same byte-for-byte dependency tree that was audited and tested. `.npmrc` in the repository root sets `package-lock=false`, disabling lockfile generation and update entirely.

**Security assessment**

Disabling the lockfile removes two security guarantees:

- Without `package-lock.json`, `npm install` resolves whatever satisfies the semver range in `package.json` at install time. A compromised registry entry for a package within the declared range is silently installed without any `git diff` in the repository.
- `npm audit` and SCA tools use the lockfile to enumerate transitive dependency versions; with `package-lock=false`, audits can only inspect direct dependencies declared in `package.json`, missing transitive-level CVEs.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-016 — Npm lifecycle scripts execute on CI/Docker install](#f-016) — without a lockfile, a compromised transitive dependency's `postinstall` script can be introduced between builds without any repository change being detectable.
- 🟠 [F-022 — Missing Security Event Logging](#f-022) — the lack of lockfile provenance means the exact package versions running in production cannot be reconstructed from the repository history for incident response.
- 🟠 [F-028 — Base image must be digest-pinned](#f-028) — the combination of floating base image and no lockfile means neither the OS layer nor the npm layer of the Docker image is reproducibly pinned.

### 7.12 Real-time and Not Applicable Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

**Controls covered:**

- [7.12.1 WebSocket / Socket.IO Security](#websocket-socket-io-security)

**Implemented controls:** `lib/startup/registerWebsocketEvents.ts` registers Socket\.IO event handlers on the shared namespace; `routes/chat.ts` serves the LLM chat channel over the same Socket\.IO connection.

**Assessment:** `Socket.IO` is present for real-time order notifications and the AI chat interface. The WebSocket upgrade path accepts connections without verifying a session token in the `io.on('connection')` handler. Broadcast events are emitted to the shared namespace without filtering by session identity, reaching unauthenticated clients. The LLM chat channel at `routes/chat.ts` adds an additional surface: prompt injection and unbounded consumption findings are primarily assessed under their respective STRIDE categories, but the Socket\.IO transport is the delivery mechanism for both.

<a id="websocket-socket-io-security"></a>
#### 7.12.1 WebSocket / Socket\.IO Security

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `Socket.IO` connections are accepted without session-token verification; broadcast events reach unauthenticated clients; the chat channel lacks per-connection rate limits.

`lib/startup/registerWebsocketEvents.ts` registers the `io.on('connection', socket => { ... })` handler on the shared `Socket.IO` namespace. The handler does not inspect `socket.handshake.auth` or `socket.handshake.headers.authorization` before registering event listeners and emitting broadcasts. `routes/chat.ts` handles the AI chat stream over the same transport.

**Security assessment**

Two weaknesses affect the Socket\.IO channel:

- `registerWebsocketEvents.ts:23`: the connection handler does not check for a valid JWT before accepting the socket into the namespace; any unauthenticated client that completes the WebSocket upgrade is treated as a connected user.
- `registerWebsocketEvents.ts:29`: order-completion and challenge-solved notifications are broadcast to all sockets in the namespace without filtering by recipient session, so an unauthenticated client receives other users' order events.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-012 — Unauthenticated WebSocket Channel](#f-012) — unauthenticated WebSocket connections are accepted without session verification (primary assessment in [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls)); the same gap enables the broadcast leakage below.
- 🟡 [F-055 — Notification Broadcast to Unauthorized Sockets](#f-055) — broadcast notifications reach unauthorized sockets because no per-socket session check is applied before the emit (primary assessment in [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls)).

### 7.13 Defense-in-Depth Summary

**Verdict:** -

Several individual controls are correctly implemented and provide genuine value. Angular's default template escaping protects the majority of data-bound output paths without developer intervention. Helmet's `noSniff()` and `frameguard()` headers eliminate two legacy browser attack classes. The JWT signing algorithm choice (RS256 asymmetric signing) is sound in principle - the weakness is key management, not the algorithm. Winston and Morgan provide structured HTTP logging that covers routine traffic. The Docker build generates a CycloneDX SBOM, giving operators a package inventory to scan against CVE databases.

The core defense-in-depth gap is that the controls protecting the three most critical boundaries - authentication, session integrity, and secret management - each fail at their foundation. Replacing the hardcoded RSA signing key with a runtime-injected secret (`e.g`. AWS Secrets Manager or a Kubernetes Secret) closes the JWT forgery path and restores the value of the RS256 choice. Switching the login and search queries from raw `models.sequelize.query()` to parameterized ORM calls removes SQL injection across both high-traffic paths. Moving the session JWT from `localStorage` to an `HttpOnly Secure SameSite=Strict` cookie (via a Backend-for-Frontend that holds the token server-side) eliminates the entire XSS-to-session-theft class. Adding a strict Content-Security-Policy and enabling the Dependabot v2 schema would then close the two remaining structural gaps in the browser and supply-chain layers.

## 8. Findings Register

Findings are grouped by severity (Critical → High → Medium → Low); within a tier they are ordered by attack vektor (Repo-Read → Internet-Anon → Internet-User → Victim-Required). Each finding is a card with the same fixed fields, in order: **Severity · Component · Location** → **Issue** → **Root cause** → **Evidence** → **Fix** → **Classification** (with external CWE / OWASP links).

<a id="th-01"></a><a id="th-02"></a><a id="th-03"></a><a id="th-06"></a><a id="th-10"></a><a id="th-04"></a><a id="th-05"></a><a id="th-07"></a><a id="th-08"></a><a id="th-09"></a><a id="th-11"></a><a id="th-12"></a><a id="th-13"></a><a id="th-14"></a><a id="th-16"></a><a id="th-17"></a><a id="th-18"></a>

### 🔴 Critical (7)

<a id="t-004"></a><a id="f-004"></a>
#### F-004 · Hardcoded RSA Signing Key (lib/insecurity.ts:21)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical - secret committed to the public source repo - extractable on clone, no prior access needed  ·  **Component:** [C-02](#c-02) - Authentication and Identity Service  ·  **Location:** `lib/insecurity.ts:21`

**Issue:** The 1024-bit RSA private key used to sign every session JWT is embedded verbatim and committed to the public OWASP Juice Shop GitHub repository. An attacker clones the repo, copies the PEM block, and signs a JWT offline with { data: { id: 1, email: 'admin@juice-`sh.op`', role: 'admin' }, algorithm: 'RS256' }.

Because `authorize()` and the express-jwt middleware both trust this key pair, the forged token is accepted as a fully authenticated admin session - no login, no SQLi, no interaction with the server needed. The same key also signs the 2FA tmpToken and the totp_setup_secret, so the attacker can mint tokens of any type.

Offline forgery of a valid session token for any user and role; the key cannot be trusted and must be rotated out of source control.

**Root cause:** Authentication can be circumvented or forged because credentials, signing keys, or password hashes are weak, missing, or exposed.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - A complete RSA PRIVATE KEY PEM literal is assigned to privateKey at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` and consumed by `authorize()` and `deluxeToken()`.

**Fix:** Move the cryptographic key out of source control into a managed secret store and rotate it → ● [M-009](#m-009) — Move cryptographic keys to a managed secret store (`insecurity.ts:21`)

**Classification:** Cryptographic Failures · [CWE-321](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/321.html) · [OWASP A02:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A02_2021/) · walkthrough [Walkthrough §3.5](#35-hardcoded-rsa-signing-key-in-authentication-and-identity-service)

<a id="t-001"></a><a id="f-001"></a>
#### F-001 · Systemic Mass Assignment of Privileged Fields (server.ts:408)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical - reaches a privileged operation on an unauthenticated endpoint  ·  **Component:** [C-03](#c-03) - Express\.js REST API Backend  ·  **Location:** `server.ts:408`

**Issue:** Self-registration is POST /api/Users, handled by the auto-generated Sequelize/finale REST endpoint. The pre-processing middleware at `server.ts:408-419` only trims email/password/passwordRepeat and never strips extra attributes, so the request body is passed through to model creation intact.

The User model (`models/user.ts:79-84`) exposes a writable role attribute whose only guard is isIn ['customer','deluxe','accounting','admin'] - which permits 'admin'. An unauthenticated attacker POSTs { email, password, passwordRepeat, role: 'admin' } and the created account is persisted with the admin role.

Any anonymous user can register a self-elevated administrator account, gaining full application control.

**Root cause:** Authorization checks are absent or bypassable, allowing horizontal and vertical privilege jumps from a self-registered or low-rights account. Includes mass-assignment of privileged attributes.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - The registration middleware at `server.ts:408` forwards the raw body to finale's create; `models/user.ts:79-84` accepts a client-supplied role including 'admin' with no server-side authority check.

```typescript
// server.ts:408
  /* Captcha Bypass challenge verification */
  app.post('/api/Feedbacks', verify.captchaBypassChallenge())
  /* User registration challenge verifications before finale takes over */
  app.post('/api/Users', (req: Request, res: Response, next: NextFunction) => {
    if (req.body.email !== undefined && req.body.password !== undefined && req.body.passwordRepeat !== undefined) {
      if (req.body.email.length !== 0 && req.body.password.length !== 0) {
        req.body.email = req.body.email.trim()
```

**Fix:** ● [M-006](#m-006) — Force role to customer on self-registration and strip client-supplied privileged (`server.ts:408`)

**Classification:** Broken Access Control · [CWE-915](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/915.html) · [OWASP A01:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A01_2021/) · walkthrough [Walkthrough §3.1](#31-systemic-mass-assignment-of-privileged-fields-in-express-js-rest-api-backend)

<a id="t-003"></a><a id="f-003"></a>
#### F-003 · OAuth password derived deterministically from email (oauth.component.ts:30)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-01](#c-01) - Angular SPA Frontend  ·  **Location:** `frontend/src/app/oauth/oauth.component.ts:30`

**Issue:** After OAuth login, OAuthComponent computes a local account password as `btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))` (`oauth.component.ts:30`, repeated at line 46) - base64 of the user's email reversed. This value is saved as the account password via `userService.save()` and reused on the normal password login endpoint.

The password is therefore a pure, public function of the email address: any attacker who knows a victim's email can compute their exact password offline (`btoa(reverse(email))`) and authenticate through the standard /rest/user/login form without ever touching the OAuth provider. This turns the email - a non-secret identifier - into the sole authentication factor and creates a parallel-auth bypass around the OAuth trust boundary.

Full account takeover of any OAuth-provisioned user by anyone who knows the target email address.

**Root cause:** Authentication can be circumvented or forged because credentials, signing keys, or password hashes are weak, missing, or exposed.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - The account password is `btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))`, a reversible deterministic transform of the public email.

```typescript
// frontend/src/app/oauth/oauth.component.ts:30
  ngOnInit (): void {
    this.userService.oauthLogin(this.parseRedirectUrlParams().access_token).subscribe({
      next: (profile: any) => {
        const password = btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))
        this.userService.save({ email: profile.email, password, passwordRepeat: password }).subscribe({
          next: () => {
            this.login(profile)
```

**Fix:** Strengthen authentication: enforce a vetted JWT verifier with explicit algorithm, MFA where appropriate → ● [M-008](#m-008) — Harden the authentication flow (`oauth.component.ts:30`)

**Classification:** OAuth / OIDC Misconfiguration · [CWE-287](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/287.html) · [OWASP A07:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A07_2021/) · walkthrough [Walkthrough §3.6](#36-oauth-password-derived-deterministically-from-email-in-oauth)

<a id="t-005"></a><a id="f-005"></a>
#### F-005 · Insecure JWT Verification (lib/insecurity.ts:55)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical - elevated as an attack-chain keystone (individual baseline: High)  ·  **Component:** [C-02](#c-02) - Authentication and Identity Service  ·  **Location:** `lib/insecurity.ts:55`

**Instances (6):** 🔴 `lib/insecurity.ts:55`, 🟢 `routes/nftMint.ts:42`, 🟠 `lib/insecurity.ts:53`, 🟠 `lib/insecurity.ts:56`, 🔴 `lib/insecurity.ts:189`, 🔴 `routes/verify.ts:120`

**Issue:** Token verification is split across two weak paths. `verify()` calls jws.verify(token, publicKey) without passing an allowed-algorithm list, and `isAuthorized()` builds express-jwt@0.1.3 with only { secret: publicKey } and no algorithms option.

Neither pins RS256. An attacker crafts a token whose header sets the algorithm to 'none' (empty signature) or to HS256 using the well-known RSA public key (encryptionkeys/jwt.pub) as the HMAC secret.

Identity spoofing of arbitrary users via alg-confusion / none-algorithm tokens, bypassing signature verification entirely.

**Root cause:** Authentication can be circumvented or forged because credentials, signing keys, or password hashes are weak, missing, or exposed.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - `insecurity.ts:52` and :55 verify JWTs with no algorithms allowlist; the public key doubles as both the RS256 verify key and (via confusion) an HS256 secret.

```typescript
// lib/insecurity.ts:55
export const isAuthorized = () => expressJwt(({ secret: publicKey }) as any)
export const denyAll = () => expressJwt({ secret: '' + Math.random() } as any)
export const authorize = (user = {}) => jwt.sign(user, privateKey, { expiresIn: '6h', algorithm: 'RS256' })
export const verify = (token: string) => token ? (jws.verify as ((token: string, secret: string) => boolean))(token, publicKey) : false
export const decode = (token: string) => { return jws.decode(token)?.payload }

export const sanitizeHtml = (html: string) => sanitizeHtmlLib(html)
```

**Fix:** Pin the signature algorithm explicitly and reject `alg:none` and unknown algorithms → ● [M-010](#m-010) — Enforce JWT signature and algorithm verification (`insecurity.ts:55`)

**Classification:** Broken Authentication · [CWE-347](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/347.html) · [OWASP A07:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A07_2021/) · walkthrough [Walkthrough §3.2](#32-insecure-jwt-verification-in-authentication-and-identity-service)

<a id="t-006"></a><a id="f-006"></a>
#### F-006 · SQL Injection in login query (routes/login.ts:34)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-02](#c-02) - Authentication and Identity Service  ·  **Location:** `routes/login.ts:34`

**Issue:** An attacker submits the login form with the email field set to ' OR 1=1--. Because `req.body.email` is concatenated directly into the query string (SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '<email>' AND password = '<md5hash>' AND deletedAt IS NULL), the injected clause short-circuits the WHERE predicate.

The query returns the first Users row - the seeded admin account - and `login.ts:47` issues a valid RS256 JWT for that user. The attacker is authenticated as admin without knowing any password.

Full authentication bypass to any account (admin included) plus blind/UNION read of the Users table.

**Root cause:** User input flows into a server-side interpreter (SQL, NoSQL, XML, YAML, LDAP, OS shell) without parameterization or schema validation.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - `req.body.email` and the md5 hash are interpolated into `models.sequelize.query` at `routes/login.ts:34` with no parameterization.

```typescript
// routes/login.ts:34

  return (req: Request, res: Response, next: NextFunction) => {
    verifyPreLoginChallenges(req) // vuln-code-snippet hide-line
    models.sequelize.query(`SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email || ''}' AND password = '${security.hash(req.body.password || '')}' AND deletedAt IS NULL`, { model: UserModel, plain: true }) // vuln-code-snippet vuln-line loginAdminChallenge loginBenderChallenge loginJimChallenge
      .then((authenticatedUser) => { // vuln-code-snippet neutral-line loginAdminChallenge loginBenderChallenge loginJimChallenge
        const user = utils.queryResultToJson(authenticatedUser)
        if (user.data?.id && user.data.totpSecret !== '') {
```

**Fix:** Switch all SQL execution to parameterised queries or ORM-bound parameters → ● [M-011](#m-011) — Use parameterized database queries (`login.ts:34`)

**Classification:** Injection · [CWE-89](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/89.html) · [OWASP A03:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A03_2021/) · walkthrough [Walkthrough §3.3](#33-sql-injection-in-login-query-in-login)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-03](#c-03) - Express\.js REST API Backend  ·  **Location:** `routes/address.ts:11`

**Instances (24):** 🟠 `routes/basketItems.ts:65`, 🟠 `server.ts:363`, 🟡 `routes/memory.ts:24`, 🟡 `routes/chat.ts:165`, 🔴 `routes/address.ts:11`, 🔴 `routes/address.ts:18`, 🔴 `routes/address.ts:29`, 🟠 `routes/basketItems.ts:68` … (+16 more)

**Issue:** Server-side authorization MUST derive the resource owner from the authenticated session (`req.user` / `req.session` / `req.auth`), never from attacker-controlled request data. Trusting `req.body.UserId` etc. enables horizontal privilege escalation across all authenticated tenants.

export function getAddress () {
